{"text":"Gram OSS RepositoryCheck out Github to see how it works under the hood, contribute improvements, or adapt it for your own use cases. Give us a star!View on GitHub\nIf you've been following the discussion around MCP (Model Context Protocol) lately, you've probably noticed the grumbling on Hacker News and elsewhere. Critics love to point out that MCP is a \"context hog,\" burning through tokens at an alarming rate when you try to expose more than a handful of tools to an AI agent.\nThis criticism reveals a fundamental tension in AI tooling: tools are incredibly useful for making LLMs productive, but letting the LLM know about those tools is context-expensive. Load up hundreds of API operations and you'll quickly hit context window limits or face prohibitive token costs.\nLots of people like to complain about the challenges of making LLMs useful and then blame it on MCP. At Speakeasy, we prefer to use the protocol to solve problems. Here are the results.\nWe've refined our Dynamic Toolset implementation for MCP servers, creating an approach that delivers the best of both worlds: the discoverability of progressive search and the efficiency of semantic search. Our latest benchmarks show up to 160x token reduction compared to static toolsets while maintaining 100% success rates across diverse tasks.\nThe new Dynamic Toolset approach reduces token usage by an average of 96% for inputs and 90% for total token consumption, making it practical to build MCP servers with hundreds of tools without overwhelming the LLM's context window. Dynamic Toolsets are available in Gram today for all MCP servers!\nLearning from our previous approach\nEarlier this month, we introduced Dynamic Toolsets with two experimental approaches:\nprogressive search and semantic search. Both delivered significant token reductions, but our production experience revealed important insights:\nSemantic search was highly effective at helping LLMs find the right tools within large toolsets using embeddings-based discovery.\nHowever, it had a critical limitation: LLMs sometimes wouldn't even attempt to search for tools because they had no visibility into what was available.\nA vague user prompt like \"how many deals do we have?\" would cause confusion because the LLM had no idea that a \"deal\" meant a \"HubSpot deal\" in this context.\nProgressive search provided excellent discoverability by including an outline of available tool \"categories\" in the tool description.\nThis helped LLMs understand what tools were available in the first place, increasing the likelihood that they would attempt to search for tools.\nHowever, this categorization depends on the quality of the input Gram Functions or OpenAPI documents. Semantic search doesn't have this limitation.\nWe also discovered that adding a separate describe_tools function significantly reduced token usage\nby allowing LLMs to retrieve input schemas only for tools they intended to use, since input schemas represent a large percentage of total tokens.\nIntroducing the refined Dynamic Toolset\nOur new approach combines the strengths of both methods into a unified system that exposes three core tools:\nsearch_tools: Uses embeddings-based semantic search like our previous approach, but now includes an overview of available tool categories in the description.\nThe LLM can also apply filters to narrow searches by tags like source:hubspot or hubspot/deals.\nThis gives the LLM both discoverability and precise search capability.\ndescribe_tools: Provides detailed schemas and documentation for specific tools. By separating this from search,\nwe optimize token usage since very large input schemas are only loaded when actually needed.\nexecute_tool: Executes the discovered and described tools. This remains unchanged but benefits from the improved discovery mechanisms.\nThe key insight is that tool descriptions provide structure and discoverability, while semantic search\nprovides efficiency and natural language discovery. Together, they create a system that scales naturally without sacrificing usability.\nPerformance results\nWe conducted extensive benchmarking across toolsets of varying sizes (40 to 400 tools)\nusing both simple single-tool tasks and complex multi-tool workflows.\nToken usage comparison\n\nSuccess ratesWe saw 100% success rates for all toolset sizes and task complexities, where\nsuccess is defined as the LLM calling the expected (underlying) tools with the\nexpected arguments. Note that we used Claude Sonnet 4.5 for all tests.\nAdvantages\nThe data reveals several significant advantages of Dynamic Toolsets:\nMassive token reduction: Input tokens are reduced by an average of 96.7% for simple tasks and 91.2% for complex tasks. Even accounting for increased output tokens from additional tool calls, total token usage drops by 96.4% and 90.7% respectively.\nConsistent scaling: Unlike static toolsets where token usage grows linearly with toolset size, Dynamic Toolsets maintain relatively constant token consumption regardless of whether you have 40 or 400 tools.\nPerfect success rates: The refined approach maintains 100% success rates across all toolset sizes and task complexities, proving that token efficiency doesn't come at the cost of reliability.\nPredictable costs: While tool calls increase by 2x on average, the dramatic reduction in token usage results in overall cost savings, especially for large toolsets where static approaches become prohibitively expensive or impossible due to context window limits.\nDisadvantages\nMore tool calls: Dynamic Toolsets require 2-3x more tool calls than a static toolset.\nThis is because the LLM needs to search for the tools it needs to use, and then describe the tools it needs to use.\nThe number of tools calls could be reduced (at the expense of token usage) by combining the search_tools and describe_tools calls into a single call.\nSlower: More tools calls means more LLM cycles, which means more time to complete the task. We saw an average of ~50% increased execution time for dynamic toolsets compared to static toolsets.\nWe expect that for longer-running agents, the execution time will be closer to (perhaps even less than) the static toolset execution time.\nThis is because as tools are discovered (brought into context), the LLM can reuse them without needing new search/describe calls.\nWhy three tool calls instead of one?\nWhile it might seem counterintuitive to increase tool calls, the three-step approach is essential for optimal token efficiency:\n\nsearch_tools: The LLM first searches for relevant tools using natural language queries\ndescribe_tools: The LLM then requests detailed schemas only for the tools it intends to use\nexecute_tool: Finally, the LLM executes the tools with the proper parameters\n\nThis separation prevents loading unnecessary schemas upfront while ensuring the LLM has complete information about the tools it chooses to use. Despite increasing tool calls by 2x, this doesn't increase total tokens or runtime because input tokens are reduced by 96%+.\nTool call patterns\nFor the \"complex\" workflow shown in our benchmarks, we typically see 6-8 total tool calls instead of the 3 tool calls required for a static toolset:\nTypical 7-call pattern (most common):\n\n3 separate search_tools calls (one for each needed tool)\n1 describe_tools call (requesting schemas for all three tools at once)\n3 execute_tool calls (one for each tool)\n\nEfficient 6-call pattern (when search is optimal):\n\n2 search_tools calls (finding multiple tools in combined searches)\n1 describe_tools call\n3 execute_tool calls\n\nExtended 8-call pattern (when search needs refinement):\n\n4 search_tools calls (one search doesn't return expected results, particularly common with larger toolsets)\n1 describe_tools call\n3 execute_tool calls\n\nEnhanced search capability: The search_tools function combines semantic search with structured filtering. Tool descriptions include categorical overviews (e.g., \"This toolset includes HubSpot CRM operations, deal management, contact synchronization...\") while maintaining the precision of embeddings-based search.\nLazy schema loading: Input schemas are only loaded via describe_tools when the LLM explicitly requests them. Since schemas often represent 60-80% of token usage in static toolsets, this separation delivers significant efficiency gains.\nIntelligent reuse: The conversation history serves as a \"cache\" for tool descriptions and schemas, reducing redundant token usage in multi-step workflows.\nWhy this approach works\nThe success of the refined Dynamic Toolsets comes from addressing the core tension between discoverability and efficiency:\nContext awareness: By including categorical overviews in tool descriptions, LLMs understand what's possible within a toolset before starting their search.\nNatural language discovery: Semantic search allows LLMs to describe their intent naturally (\"find tools for managing customer deals\") rather than guessing exact function names.\nJust-in-time loading: Only the tools and schemas actually needed for a task consume tokens, keeping context windows focused and efficient.\nScalable architecture: Adding new tools to a toolset doesn't increase initial token usage, making the approach naturally scalable for growing APIs.\nGetting started\nDynamic Toolsets are available in Gram for all MCP servers. To enable them:\n\nNavigate to the MCP section in your Gram dashboard\nSelect your toolset and switch from \"Static\" to \"Dynamic\" mode\nConfigure any tag-based filters if your toolset includes categorized tools\n\nThe system handles the transition automatically, maintaining full compatibility with existing MCP implementations while delivering immediate token efficiency benefits.\nThe bigger picture\nMCP gets a lot of criticism, but here's the thing: it's just an implementation detail. At its core, MCP is a flexible protocol that gets out of the way to let you solve real problems. The complaints you hear aren't really about the protocol—they're about the fundamental challenge of building useful AI agents at scale.\nYou could build specialized \"code mode\" environments that sidestep context limitations entirely, and some teams do. But those solutions require significant engineering investment and domain expertise that most teams simply don't have. The beauty of MCP is that it provides a standardized way to make any API accessible to AI agents, and approaches like Dynamic Toolsets show how the protocol can adapt to solve its own scaling challenges.\nDynamic Toolsets represent more than just a token optimization—they demonstrate that MCP can evolve to meet the practical needs of production AI systems. By treating the context window as a resource to be managed rather than a hard constraint to work around, we've opened up new possibilities for building comprehensive AI agents that can work with complex enterprise systems.\nThe future of AI tooling isn't about building perfect protocols from day one. It's about building flexible systems that can adapt as we learn more about what works in practice. MCP provides that flexibility, and we're just getting started.\nAdditional reading\n\nPrevious Dynamic Toolsets implementation\nDynamic Toolsets documentation\nCode execution with MCP\nTool design best practices\n","url":"https://www.speakeasy.com/blog/how-we-reduced-token-usage-by-100x-dynamic-toolsets-v2"}
{"text":"LLM APIs are priced by the token. Every token you send and receive has a cost. As you scale from prototype to production — from tens of requests to thousands per day — the difference between optimized and unoptimized token usage can be tens of thousands of dollars per year.\nThis guide covers the full landscape of LLM token optimization strategies. It's based on research across Anthropic's documentation, real-world usage data, and academic findings on retrieval-augmented generation and long-context performance.\nThe core thesis: token optimization is a context-engineering problem, not a prompt-shortening problem. Most teams waste time making prompts shorter when the real cost drivers are bloated context, idle tool schemas, and stale conversation history.\nWhy token optimization matters now\nThree trends make token optimization increasingly important:\n\nPricing tiers: Anthropic's current models (Opus 4.8, Opus 4.7, Opus 4.6, and Sonnet 4.6) include the full 1M-token context window at standard pricing — the long-context surcharge was eliminated in March 2026. Claude Opus 4.8 is now the most capable Opus-tier model (released May 28, 2026) at the same $5/$25/MTok pricing as Opus 4.7/4.6. Above that, Claude Fable 5 launched June 9, 2026 as Anthropic's most-capable widely-released model at $10/$50/MTok with a 1M context window (Mythos 5 sits beside it as invitation-only via Project Glasswing). The Sonnet 4 and Opus 4 models retire June 15, 2026 — migrate to Sonnet 4.6 and Opus 4.8 respectively. Opus 4.1 retires August 5, 2026. Claude Haiku 3 was retired April 20, 2026; migrate to Haiku 4.5. OpenAI's flagship is GPT-5.5 at $5/$30 per MTok with 90% cached-input discounts ($0.50/MTok cached input) — but GPT-5.5 Pro ($30/$180/MTok) offers no cached-input discount. The prior GPT-5.4 family ($2.50/$15, mini at $0.75/$4.50, nano at $0.20/$1.25) remains available, also with 90% cached-input discounts. One important caveat: Opus 4.7 and 4.8 (and Fable 5/Mythos 5) ship with a new tokenizer that uses up to 35% more tokens for the same text vs Opus 4.6 (more pronounced for code and structured data, negligible for plain English) — factor this in when migrating or estimating costs.\nAgent architectures: Coding agents, tool-use workflows, and multi-step reasoning all multiply token usage. A single agent session can consume 10–100x more tokens than a simple API call.\nDiminishing returns on long context: Research consistently shows that relevant information buried in the middle of a long context is used less reliably. More tokens doesn't just cost more — it can produce worse results.\n\nThe strategies below are organized from highest to lowest ROI for most teams.\n1. Context engineering and session management\nThe single biggest source of token waste in LLM applications is context bloat — sending far more context than the model needs for the current step.\nThe key strategies:\n\nSplit work into phases: Do discovery, implementation, and verification in separate sessions. Stale context from failed attempts charges you on every subsequent turn while degrading quality.\nJust-in-time retrieval: Pull in exactly the information needed, right when it's needed. Targeted file reads and LSP navigation beat repo dumps. Research on iterative repository retrieval (RepoCoder) showed >10% improvement in accuracy over in-file completion while using less context.\nRepo memory: Put durable project knowledge (architecture, conventions, build commands) in structured config files like CLAUDE.md that load automatically, rather than typing it into every conversation.\nServer-side context summarization (Compaction API): Anthropic's Compaction API (beta, February 2026) enables Opus 4.6, 4.7, and 4.8 to automatically summarize and compress conversation history, enabling effectively infinite conversations without manual context trimming or session resets.\nPersistent memory and Dreaming: For agents that work across many sessions, Anthropic's Memory tool (public beta, April 2026) and Dreaming feature (announced May 2026) let agents keep curated facts in a server-side filesystem and consolidate them asynchronously between sessions — replacing tens of thousands of replayed-history tokens with a compact memory load on each new session.\n\nThis is the single most impactful optimization for most teams. Read the full deep dive: Context Engineering: Why Reducing Token Usage Isn't About Shorter Prompts\n2. Provider-specific API techniques\nEach LLM provider has features specifically designed to reduce costs. Most developers don't use them, or use them incorrectly.\nThe key strategies:\n\nPrompt caching: Anthropic cache reads cost 0.1x base input price — a 90% discount. Anthropic now supports automatic caching for multi-turn conversations (a single top-level cache_control field manages breakpoints automatically), alongside the existing explicit breakpoint approach. GPT-5.5 and GPT-5.4 both offer 90% cached-input discounts, matching Anthropic's rate (exception: GPT-5.5 Pro has no cached-input discount).\nAdvisor Tool (April 2026 beta): Pair a cheap executor model (Sonnet 4.6 or Haiku 4.5) with Opus 4.6/4.7/4.8 as a high-intelligence advisor that's only consulted when needed. Typical coding agent sessions run 73–87% cheaper than Opus solo, since most turns are handled at Sonnet/Haiku rates and the advisor generates only 400–700 tokens per consultation. Note: if using Opus 4.7 or 4.8 as advisor, account for the new tokenizer when estimating those advisor-turn costs.\nSuppress thinking output: Claude 4.6 models support thinking.display: \"omitted\" to strip reasoning traces from API responses. The model still reasons internally; you just don't pay output tokens for traces you'll discard.\nStructured outputs: Tool schemas and JSON mode eliminate the retry loops caused by malformed responses. Every eliminated retry is a full API call you didn't pay for.\nBatch APIs: All major providers (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google) offer 50% savings for non-time-sensitive workloads.\nOutput constraints: Set realistic max_tokens, ask for diffs instead of full rewrites, and use stop sequences.\n\nRead the full deep dive: How to Reduce OpenAI and Claude API Token Costs\n3. Tool and schema overhead reduction\nA source of waste most developers don't know about: tool definitions are included in every API request. Real-world setups have measured 55K–134K tokens of tool-definition overhead before any work starts.\nThe key strategies:\n\nDisable unused MCP servers: Each server's tool definitions load on every request whether you use them or not.\nOn-demand tool loading: Use a tool-search pattern to load tools only when needed. This reduced one setup's overhead from 134K to 8.7K tokens — an 85% reduction.\nPrefer CLI tools: When a direct command-line tool does the job, it avoids the schema overhead of the MCP layer.\nProgressive disclosure: Use Skills or equivalent patterns where full instructions load only when triggered.\n\nRead the full deep dive: Cut MCP and Tool Overhead to Save Thousands of Tokens Per Request\n4. Prompt cache architecture\nCaching isn't a toggle — it's an architecture. Most teams enable prompt caching but get low hit rates because their prompts aren't designed for it.\nThe key strategies:\n\nStable prefix pattern: Put stable content (system instructions, tool definitions) first and variable content (user input) last.\nMulti-tier caching: Use breakpoints to cache sections that change at different rates independently.\nAvoid cache busters: Timestamps in system prompts, shuffled few-shot examples, and dynamic tool lists all destroy cache hit rates.\n\nRead the full deep dive: Designing for Prompt Cache Hits: How to Save 90% on Input Tokens\n5. Model routing and right-sizing\nNot every task needs your most expensive model. A routing layer that sends easy tasks to cheap models and hard tasks to expensive ones can cut costs by 40–60%.\nThe key strategies:\n\nTask-based routing: Classification, extraction, and formatting go to small models (Haiku 4.5, gpt-5.4-nano at $0.20/MTok). Complex reasoning and architecture decisions go to large models (Opus 4.8, Opus 4.7, GPT-5.5 Pro). For the absolute hardest reasoning at scale, Claude Fable 5 ($10/$50/MTok) sits above the Opus tier. Notably, o3 dropped 80% in price (April 2026) to $2/$8/MTok, making strong reasoning available at mid-tier cost — worth reassessing if you previously skipped it due to price. When routing to Opus 4.7/4.8 or Fable 5, validate token budgets first — the new tokenizer adds up to 35% more tokens vs Opus 4.6 for code-heavy inputs.\nThinking/effort controls: Extended thinking burns output tokens (the expensive kind). Dial it down for simple tasks.\nSubagent model selection: Route simple subagent work to cheaper models. Agent teams use ~7x more tokens than standard sessions, so model choice matters more.\n\nRead more: 5 Ways to Reduce Your LLM API Costs Today\n6. Measurement and monitoring\nYou can't optimize what you can't measure. And most teams optimize the wrong thing because they haven't measured where their tokens actually go.\nThe key strategies:\n\nUse built-in tools: Claude Code's /cost, /context, and /mcp commands reveal real-time token usage.\nAPI-level tracking: Token Count API (pre-flight checks) and Usage & Cost API (post-hoc breakdowns by model, cache, and context tier).\nFind the real hotspots: Research shows that review and rework loops consume ~59% of tokens on average — not the initial generation. Input context growth, not prompt size, is usually the main cost driver.\n\nRead the full deep dive: How to Measure and Monitor LLM Token Usage\n7. Token-efficient prompting patterns\nHow you prompt the model — and what format you request — can dramatically affect token usage independently of context size.\nThe key strategies:\n\nChain of Draft (CoD): A prompting technique that matches Chain of Thought accuracy while using as little as 7.6% of the reasoning tokens. Instead of verbose step-by-step reasoning, the model drafts each step in ~5 words.\nOutput format optimization: JSON uses approximately 2x more tokens than YAML or TSV for the same data. For internal pipelines where compatibility isn't critical, switching formats can halve structured output costs.\nPrompt compression: Tools like LLMLingua can compress prompts by up to 20x while preserving the model's ability to answer correctly — especially effective for RAG pipelines with long retrieved chunks.\nSemantic caching: Application-level caching that matches semantically similar queries (not just exact prefixes), avoiding the API call entirely for repeated question types.\n\nRead the full deep dive: Token-Efficient Prompting Patterns: Chain of Draft, Output Formats, and Prompt Compression\nThe 3 highest-ROI changes\nIf you only have time for three optimizations, research and production data suggest these deliver the most impact:\n\n\nSpec in one session, implement in a fresh one. Resetting context between phases eliminates the compounding cost of stale history. This is free to implement and immediately reduces token usage on every subsequent turn.\n\n\nReplace repo dumps with targeted retrieval. Use code intelligence, LSP navigation, and focused file reads instead of dumping entire files or directories into context. Less context, better results, lower cost.\n\n\nPrune tools and MCP servers, then rely on caching for the stable remainder. Disable unused servers, switch to on-demand tool loading, and make sure your remaining tool definitions are cache-friendly. This attacks the constant overhead that charges you on every single request.\n\n\nThese three target the recurring token leaks that appear on almost every turn: stale history, irrelevant code context, and idle tool schemas.\nThese strategies transfer across providers\nWhile the examples in this guide reference Claude and OpenAI specifically, the underlying problems — finite attention, long-context degradation, retrieval vs. dumping, and tool-schema overhead — are not provider-specific. The same strategies apply to Gemini, Codex, and any other LLM-based tool or API.\nThe fundamentals don't change: send the right context at the right time, measure where your tokens go, and optimize the actual hotspots.\nReferences\n\nAnthropic: Claude Pricing — Pricing tiers and cache rates\nAnthropic: Models overview — Current lineup including Opus 4.8 and Claude Fable 5\nAnthropic: Token-Saving Updates — Tool overhead analysis (55K–134K tokens) and on-demand loading results\nRepoCoder: Repository-Level Code Completion — Iterative retrieval vs in-file completion\nLost in the Middle: How Language Models Use Long Contexts — Diminishing returns of long context\nChain of Draft (CoD) — Matching CoT accuracy with 7.6% of the tokens\nOpenAI: API Pricing — Model pricing and batch API rates\n\n\nFor more specific topics, explore our other guides:\n\nClaude Code efficiency tips\nContext engineering\nReducing OpenAI and Claude API costs\nCutting tool overhead\nDesigning for prompt cache hits\nToken-efficient prompting patterns\nMeasuring and monitoring token usage\n","url":"https://www.tokenoptimize.dev/guides/llm-token-optimization-strategies"}
{"text":"  MCP token optimization addresses two distinct costs that consume an AI agent’s context window: schema bloat and response bloat.\nSchema bloat is the token cost of loading tool definitions into context. GitHub’s official MCP server consumes 17,600 tokens of tool definitions per request. Connect multiple servers and you reach 30,000+ tokens of metadata before the agent does any work.\nResponse bloat is the token cost of tool outputs flowing back through context. A single HRIS API call can return hundreds of thousands of characters of raw JSON. Response bloat often consumes more context than schema bloat but receives less attention.\nMost optimization approaches address only one side. This post compares four approaches, what each solves, and the sourced benchmarks behind them.\nMCP Schema Compression: Reduce Tool Definition Tokens\nMCP schema compression reduces the token cost of tool definitions by stripping descriptions, enums, and nested type documentation while preserving the parameter structure agents need to call the tool.\nAtlassian’s mcp-compressor is an open-source proxy that implements this approach. It sits between your MCP servers and the LLM, offering four compression levels that trade description richness for token savings.\nGitHub MCP server (94 tools) at each level:\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nLevelWhat’s keptTokensReductionNoneFull descriptions, enums, types17,600—LowFull descriptions preserved~3,90078%Medium (default)First sentence of each description~3,30081%HighTool names + parameter names only~2,20088%MaxOnly a list_tools() function~50097%\nSource: Atlassian, benchmarked on GitHub’s official MCP server.\nThe tradeoff is accuracy. At high compression, a tool called create_jira_issue has no description. Only parameter names remain. If another tool called create_confluence_page shares the same parameter names (title, description, project), the model has to guess which one creates a Jira task and which creates a Confluence wiki page.\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nSolvesDoesn’t solveSchema compressionSchema bloat. Drop-in proxy, no changes to servers or agent logic.Response bloat. And over-compression hurts tool selection accuracy.\nWhen to use schema compression: Teams running multiple MCP servers who need low-effort token reduction. Start at medium compression, benchmark tool selection accuracy on real tasks, then tighten where accuracy holds.\nMCP Tool Search: On-Demand Schema Discovery\nSearch-first tool discovery replaces upfront schema loading with on-demand retrieval. The agent starts with 2-3 lightweight meta-tools (search and execute) instead of the full tool catalog. When it needs a capability, it searches by natural language, retrieves only the matching tool definitions, and executes. The full catalog never enters context.\nImplementations vary, but the architecture is the same:\n\nClaude Code Tool Search — activates automatically when tool descriptions exceed 10% of context. Replaces the schema dump with a searchable registry. (Anthropic)\nSpeakeasy Dynamic Toolsets — three meta-tools (search_tools, describe_tools, execute_tool). The agent discovers capabilities conversationally.\nStackOne Tool Search — searchTools() uses semantic search (with local BM25+TF-IDF fallback) across 200+ connectors and 10,000+ actions. The agent sees exactly 2 tools (tool_search + tool_execute) regardless of catalog size.\n\nThe numbers from Speakeasy’s benchmarks:\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nScenarioStatic toolsetDynamic toolsetReductionSimple task (400 tools)Over 400,000 tokens~6,000 tokens98.5%Complex task (400 tools)Over 400,000 tokens~35,000 tokens91.2%200 tools loaded statically261,700 tokens—Exceeds Claude’s 200K context window\nSource: Speakeasy published benchmarks.\nAccuracy improves too. With fewer irrelevant tools in context, models pick the right tool more often:\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nModelWithout Tool SearchWith Tool SearchGainSourceOpus 449%74%+25 percentage pointsAnthropicOpus 4.579.5%88.1%+8.6 percentage pointsAnthropic\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nSolvesDoesn’t solveSearch-first discoverySchema bloat. Context stays constant regardless of catalog size. Better tool selection accuracy.Response bloat. Once a tool executes, the full output flows back. Also adds latency (~50% more round trips per tool call).\nWhen to use search-first discovery: Catalogs with 50+ tools where loading all definitions upfront is impractical. StackOne uses this pattern in production across 200+ connectors and 10,000+ actions.\nMCP Response Filtering: Reduce Tool Output Tokens\nMCP response filtering reduces the token cost of tool outputs by requesting only the fields the agent needs, rather than returning full API payloads. Where schema compression and search-first discovery address input tokens, response filtering addresses output tokens.\nConsider an HRIS list_employees call that returns 50 fields per record — name, email, SSN, benefits, compensation history, emergency contacts, manager chain. The agent needs 5 fields. The other 45 consume context without contributing to the task.\nField selection trims the response before it hits the agent:\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nIllustrative exampleFull API response (50 fields × 20 records)Hundreds of thousands of charactersFiltered response (5 fields × 20 records)~8,000 charactersReduction~95%+ per call\nNote: Exact sizes vary by provider and record count. The pattern is consistent — unfiltered HRIS/CRM responses are an order of magnitude larger than what the agent needs.\nMCP servers that support field selection — like GraphQL-based implementations or servers built with response shaping in mind — can apply this filtering server-side before the response reaches the agent.\nBut filtering doesn’t solve accumulation. Even with 5 fields per call, tokens add up across a multi-step workflow:\n\nStep 1: ~2K tokens\nStep 2: ~2K tokens\nStep 3: ~3K tokens\nAfter 5 steps: 12-15K tokens of tool responses in context\n\nThis triggers what Stanford researchers call the “lost in the middle” problem — LLM performance degrades by over 20% when relevant information sits in the middle of long context rather than at the beginning or end. Responses from earlier steps may be effectively ignored by the time the agent reaches step 5.\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nSolvesDoesn’t solveResponse filteringResponse bloat on a per-call basis. Essential hygiene for any MCP server returning raw API payloads.Accumulation across multi-step workflows. Also requires the MCP server to support field selection — many don’t.\nWhen to use response filtering: Any integration where tool responses contain more data than the agent needs. Particularly important for HRIS, CRM, and ATS integrations where API responses include dozens of fields per record.\nMCP Code Execution: Eliminate Schema and Response Bloat\nCode-based execution (also called Code Mode) replaces MCP tool schemas and raw API responses with a single execute_code tool. It is the only approach that addresses both schema bloat and response bloat in one architectural move.\nHow it works. Instead of loading tool schemas into the agent’s context, a gateway gives the agent a sandboxed code execution environment running in a V8 isolate (for example, on Cloudflare Workers). The agent discovers available APIs, writes code against them, and only the filtered result comes back into context.\nThe agent does not write code from nothing. The gateway exposes API definitions that the agent inspects before writing code.\n\nAt StackOne, Code Mode implements this as a gateway in front of any remote MCP server. The agent uses execute_code to run JavaScript in a V8 isolate, search_tools to discover available functions at runtime, and .__schema() to inspect input parameters before calling. Credentials are encrypted with AES-256-GCM and injected at the transport layer so the LLM never sees them. It works with any MCP-compatible client: Claude Code, Cursor, VS Code, Windsurf, Gemini CLI, and others.\nAnthropic and Cloudflare have published similar patterns. Anthropic’s sandbox exposes TypeScript function signatures the agent reads before writing code. Cloudflare’s exposes a pre-resolved OpenAPI spec the agent navigates with a search() tool. The architectural principle is the same across all three.\n// StackOne Code Mode — the agent writes this after discovering\n// the HRIS API via search_tools and inspecting via .__schema().\n// It runs in a V8 sandbox at the edge, not in the agent's context.\nemployees\n  .filter({ dept: 'Engineering' })\n  .sortBy('perf_score', 'desc')\n  .pick(['name', 'perf_score', 'manager'])\n  .take(10)\n// 10 rows come back. ~312 characters. The full employee list never enters context.\nSourced benchmarks:\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nImplementationBeforeAfterReductionSourceStackOne Code Mode55,000+ chars schema416 chars99.3%StackOneAnthropic (spreadsheet filtering)150,000 tokens2,000 tokens98.7%Anthropic engineering blogCloudflare (2,500 API endpoints → 2 tools)1,170,000 tokens~1,000 tokens99.9%Cloudflare blog\nAccuracy impact. StackOne benchmarked Code Mode against the MCP Atlas evaluation suite. The results show Code Mode significantly improves agent tool-use accuracy:\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nModelNative MCP+ StackOne Code ModeSourceSonnet 4.642%80%StackOne (MCP Atlas benchmark)Opus 4.662%—StackOne (MCP Atlas benchmark)\nA cheaper model with Code Mode outperforms the flagship model without it. The improvement comes from freeing context for reasoning. When tool schemas and verbose responses no longer consume 90%+ of the context window, the model has more capacity to select the right tool and execute correctly.\nWhat code execution requires:\n\nA sandboxed runtime. V8 isolates, Cloudflare Workers, or containerized environments with resource limits and monitoring.\nAPI discoverability. The agent needs to discover available functions and inspect their schemas within the sandbox before writing code.\nCredential injection. Credentials must be injected at the transport layer so the agent’s code can authenticate with APIs without the LLM ever seeing secrets.\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nSolvesDoesn’t solveCode-based executionBoth schema bloat and response bloat. Fixed context footprint regardless of API surface. 96-99% token reduction.Requires sandbox infrastructure. Higher setup complexity than the other three approaches. Agent must generate correct code.\nWhen to use code-based execution: Workflows where both schema and response bloat contribute to context pressure. Particularly effective for large API surfaces (hundreds of endpoints) and data-heavy connectors like BambooHR, Salesforce, and Workday.\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nApproachSchema reductionResponse reductionSetup costBest forSchema Compression70-97%NoneLow (drop-in proxy)Quick wins on multi-server setupsSearch-First Discovery91-97%NoneLow-MediumLarge catalogs (50+ tools)Response FilteringNone~95% per callMediumLarge API responses (HRIS, CRM)Code-Based Execution98-99%97-99%HighBoth problems at scale\nHow to Choose an MCP Token Optimization Strategy\nWhen tool schemas consume too much context\n\nStart with search-first discovery. Claude Code enables this automatically. StackOne’s getSearchTool() gives the agent 2 tools regardless of catalog size.\nAdd schema compression as a drop-in proxy for additional reduction.\n\nWhen tool responses are too large\n\nResponse filtering is essential. Build field selection into your MCP server or use one that supports it.\n\nWhen both schemas and responses are the problem\n\nCode-based execution (Code Mode) addresses both in one architectural move. Higher setup cost, but fixed context footprint regardless of API surface.\n\nWhen combining multiple approaches\n\nSearch-first discovery + response filtering covers most use cases.\nAdd Code Mode for the highest-volume workflows where both sides matter.\n\nA common gap is optimizing schema bloat while leaving response bloat unaddressed. Measuring both input and output token costs before selecting an approach avoids solving the smaller problem.\nAt StackOne, we use search-first discovery across 200+ connectors and 10,000+ actions to keep schema costs constant regardless of catalog size. StackOne Code Mode goes further, eliminating both schema and response bloat through sandboxed code execution at the edge. For a deeper look at context window failure patterns, see six patterns that kill agent context windows.  ","url":"https://www.stackone.com/blog/mcp-token-optimization/"}
{"text":"\n                \n  \n  \n  Complete Guide to MCP (Model Context Protocol) in 2026 — Architecture, Implementation, and Enterprise Roadmap for AI Agent Integration\n\n\nIn November 2024, Anthropic open-sourced the Model Context Protocol (MCP), and in just 18 months it has become the de facto standard for AI agent integration. As of March 2026, MCP has surpassed 97 million monthly SDK downloads, earned over 81,000 GitHub stars, and is supported by every major AI vendor — Anthropic, OpenAI, Google, Microsoft, and AWS. MCP standardizes how AI models interact with external tools, data sources, and systems, earning the nickname \"the USB-C of the AI world.\"\n\nThis guide covers everything you need to deploy MCP in production: core architecture, Streamable HTTP transport, OAuth 2.1 authentication, FastMCP server implementation, A2A protocol comparison, and the 2026 enterprise roadmap.\n\n\n  \n  \n  MCP Core Architecture — Host, Client, Server Three-Layer Model\n\n\nMCP uses a client-server architecture built on JSON-RPC 2.0. A single host application (Claude Desktop, Claude Code, Cursor, etc.) creates multiple isolated MCP client sessions, each maintaining a stateful JSON-RPC channel with its own MCP server.\n\n\n\n\nComponent\nRole\nExamples\n\n\n\n\nMCP Host\nAI application providing LLM integration\nClaude Desktop, Claude Code, Cursor, Cline\n\n\nMCP Client\nConnector created by the host, managing 1:1 sessions with servers\nInternal process (not exposed to users)\n\n\nMCP Server\nService providing external context and capabilities\nGitHub, Slack, PostgreSQL, Notion MCP servers\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  Three Server Primitives\n\n\nMCP servers expose external capabilities through three core primitives. Tools are functions the model can invoke (e.g., file search, DB queries, API calls). Resources are data sources the model can read (e.g., file contents, database records). Prompts are pre-defined templates that guide user workflows.\n\n\n\n# MCP Server Three Primitives — FastMCP Example\nfrom fastmcp import FastMCP\n\nmcp = FastMCP(\"my-service\")\n\n# 1. Tool — Functions the model can call\n@mcp.tool()\ndef search_database(query: str, limit: int = 10) -> list[dict]:\n    \"\"\"Search the database by keyword.\"\"\"\n    return db.search(query, limit=limit)\n\n# 2. Resource — Data the model can read\n@mcp.resource(\"config://app-settings\")\ndef get_settings() -> str:\n    \"\"\"Return application settings.\"\"\"\n    return json.dumps(current_settings)\n\n# 3. Prompt — Pre-defined workflow templates\n@mcp.prompt()\ndef analyze_data(dataset_name: str) -> str:\n    \"\"\"Guide data analysis workflow.\"\"\"\n    return f\"Please analyze the following dataset: {dataset_name}\"\n\n\n\n    Enter fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n    Exit fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  Transport Layer — Evolution from stdio to Streamable HTTP\n\n\nMCP supports two transport modes. stdio handles local inter-process communication and is the default for running local MCP servers in Claude Desktop or Claude Code. Streamable HTTP, introduced in the November 2025 spec, replaces the legacy SSE (Server-Sent Events) transport and enables MCP servers to run as remote services.\n\n\n\n\nAspect\nstdio\nStreamable HTTP\n\n\n\n\nDeployment\nLocal process (same machine as host)\nRemote service (cloud-deployable)\n\n\nScalability\nSingle instance\nHorizontal scaling with load balancers\n\n\nAuthentication\nOS-level process isolation\nOAuth 2.1 + PKCE\n\n\nUse Cases\nDeveloper tools, local file access\nSaaS integrations, enterprise services\n\n\n2026 Status\nStable (default in most IDEs)\nSSE officially deprecated, migrating to Streamable HTTP\n\n\n\n\nThe key evolution on the 2026 roadmap is stateless operation. Current MCP servers must maintain session state, which limits horizontal scaling behind load balancers. The new spec standardizes session creation, resumption, and migration so server restarts and scale-out events are transparent to connected clients.\n\n\n  \n  \n  OAuth 2.1 Authentication and Enterprise Security\n\n\nRemote MCP servers adopted OAuth 2.1 as the authentication standard starting with the June 2025 spec. MCP servers are classified as OAuth Resource Servers and advertise their authorization server location through .well-known endpoints. Clients must implement RFC 8707 Resource Indicators to prevent token misuse attacks.\n\n\n\n# MCP Server OAuth 2.1 Authentication Flow Configuration\n# .well-known/mcp-server-metadata.json\nauthorization_endpoint: \"https://auth.example.com/authorize\"\ntoken_endpoint: \"https://auth.example.com/token\"\nregistration_endpoint: \"https://auth.example.com/register\"\n\n# OAuth 2.1 Client Requirements\ngrant_types_supported:\n  - authorization_code   # PKCE required\ncode_challenge_methods_supported:\n  - S256                  # SHA-256 hash (plain forbidden)\ntoken_endpoint_auth_methods_supported:\n  - none                  # Public client (browser-based agents)\n  - client_secret_post    # Confidential client (server-side)\n\n# Resource Indicator (RFC 8707) — Token scope restriction\nresource: \"https://mcp.example.com/v1\"\nscope: \"tools:read tools:execute resources:read\"\n\n\n\n    Enter fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n    Exit fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\nThe Q2 2026 enterprise authentication roadmap adds PKCE flows for browser-based agents and SAML/OIDC integration for enterprise identity providers. Q4 brings the MCP Registry — a curated, verified server directory with security audits, usage statistics, and SLA commitments.\n\n\n  \n  \n  Building Production MCP Servers with FastMCP\n\n\nFastMCP dramatically simplifies Python-based MCP server development. FastMCP 3.0, released January 2026, handles schema generation, validation, and documentation with a single decorator. For TypeScript, combine @modelcontextprotocol/sdk with Zod for type-safe server construction.\n\n\n\n# FastMCP 3.0 — Production MCP Server Example (Python)\nfrom fastmcp import FastMCP\nfrom pydantic import BaseModel, Field\nimport httpx\n\nmcp = FastMCP(\n    \"weather-service\",\n    description=\"MCP server providing real-time weather information\",\n)\n\nclass WeatherResult(BaseModel):\n    city: str = Field(description=\"City name\")\n    temperature: float = Field(description=\"Current temperature (Celsius)\")\n    condition: str = Field(description=\"Weather condition\")\n    humidity: int = Field(description=\"Humidity (%)\")\n\n@mcp.tool()\nasync def get_weather(city: str) -> WeatherResult:\n    \"\"\"Query current weather for a specified city.\n\n    Args:\n        city: City name to query (e.g., Seoul, Tokyo)\n    \"\"\"\n    async with httpx.AsyncClient() as client:\n        resp = await client.get(\n            f\"https://api.weather.example/v1/current?city={city}\"\n        )\n        data = resp.json()\n    return WeatherResult(\n        city=data[\"city\"],\n        temperature=data[\"temp_c\"],\n        condition=data[\"condition\"],\n        humidity=data[\"humidity\"],\n    )\n\n# Resource — Supported city list\n@mcp.resource(\"weather://supported-cities\")\ndef supported_cities() -> str:\n    \"\"\"Return list of supported cities.\"\"\"\n    return \"Seoul, Tokyo, New York, London, Paris, Berlin\"\n\nif __name__ == \"__main__\":\n    mcp.run()  # stdio mode (default) or --transport http for remote mode\n\n\n\n    Enter fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n    Exit fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n// TypeScript MCP Server — @modelcontextprotocol/sdk + Zod\nimport { McpServer } from \"@modelcontextprotocol/sdk/server/mcp.js\";\nimport { StdioServerTransport } from \"@modelcontextprotocol/sdk/server/stdio.js\";\nimport { z } from \"zod\";\n\nconst server = new McpServer({\n  name: \"weather-service\",\n  version: \"1.0.0\",\n});\n\n// Tool registration — Type-safe schema with Zod\nserver.tool(\n  \"get_weather\",\n  \"Query current weather for a specified city\",\n  { city: z.string().describe(\"City name\") },\n  async ({ city }) => {\n    const data = await fetchWeather(city);\n    return { content: [{ type: \"text\", text: JSON.stringify(data) }] };\n  }\n);\n\nconst transport = new StdioServerTransport();\nawait server.connect(transport);\n\n\n\n    Enter fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n    Exit fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  Connecting MCP Servers in Claude Desktop/Code\n\n\n\n\n\n// claude_desktop_config.json — MCP Server Connection Settings\n{\n  \"mcpServers\": {\n    \"weather\": {\n      \"command\": \"python\",\n      \"args\": [\"-m\", \"weather_service\"],\n      \"env\": {\n        \"WEATHER_API_KEY\": \"your-api-key\"\n      }\n    },\n    \"github\": {\n      \"command\": \"npx\",\n      \"args\": [\"-y\", \"@modelcontextprotocol/server-github\"],\n      \"env\": {\n        \"GITHUB_PERSONAL_ACCESS_TOKEN\": \"ghp_...\"\n      }\n    },\n    \"remote-service\": {\n      \"url\": \"https://mcp.example.com/v1\"\n      // Streamable HTTP — remote MCP server (OAuth handled automatically)\n    }\n  }\n}\n\n\n\n    Enter fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n    Exit fullscreen mode\n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  MCP Ecosystem Status — 200+ Servers and Key Integrations\n\n\nAs of March 2026, the MCP ecosystem includes over 200 server implementations. Most major SaaS platforms and developer tools provide MCP servers, including GitHub, Slack, Google Drive, PostgreSQL, Notion, Jira, and Salesforce.\n\n\n\n\nCategory\nMajor MCP Servers\nCapabilities\n\n\n\n\nDev Tools\nGitHub, GitLab, Sentry\nIssue/PR management, code search, error tracking\n\n\nCollaboration\nSlack, Notion, Google Drive\nMessage search, document read/write, file management\n\n\nDatabases\nPostgreSQL, MongoDB, Redis\nSchema exploration, query execution, data analysis\n\n\nCloud/Infra\nAWS, Kubernetes, Terraform\nResource inspection, cluster management, IaC validation\n\n\nCRM/Business\nSalesforce, Jira, HubSpot\nLead management, ticket handling, pipeline analysis\n\n\nIDE Integration\nClaude Code, Cursor, Cline, Windsurf\nCode editing, terminal execution, filesystem access\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  MCP vs A2A — AI Agent Protocol Comparison\n\n\nA frequently discussed companion to MCP is A2A (Agent-to-Agent), released by Google in April 2025. The two protocols are complementary, not competing. MCP defines \"how agents interact with tools,\" while A2A defines \"how agents collaborate with each other.\" In December 2025, Anthropic, Block (Square), and OpenAI established the Agentic AI Foundation under the Linux Foundation, contributing both MCP and A2A.\n\n\n\n\nComparison\nMCP (Model Context Protocol)\nA2A (Agent-to-Agent)\n\n\n\n\nPurpose\nAgent ↔ Tool/data connection\nAgent ↔ Agent collaboration\n\n\nProtocol Base\nJSON-RPC 2.0\nJSON-RPC 2.0 + gRPC (v1.0)\n\n\nDiscovery\nMCP Server Cards (.well-known)\nAgent Cards (signed)\n\n\nAuthentication\nOAuth 2.1 + PKCE\nOAuth 2.1 + multi-tenancy\n\n\nGovernance\nAgentic AI Foundation (Linux Foundation)\nAgentic AI Foundation (Linux Foundation)\n\n\n2026 Adoption\n97M+ monthly downloads, 200+ servers\nv1.0 released, gRPC support added\n\n\nAnalogy\n\"USB-C\" — device connection standard\n\"TCP/IP\" — device communication standard\n\n\n\n\nIn practice, MCP and A2A are used together. Individual agents access their tools and data via MCP, while task delegation and result sharing between agents happens through A2A. For example, a customer support agent queries CRM and knowledge bases via MCP, then delegates complex technical issues to a technical support agent through A2A.\n\n\n  \n  \n  2026 MCP Roadmap — Enterprise Production Ready\n\n\nMCP's 2026 roadmap prioritizes enterprise production readiness. Key working group milestones:\n\n\n\n\nTimeline\nArea\nKey Deliverables\n\n\n\n\nQ1-Q2 2026\nTransport Evolution\nStreamable HTTP stateless operation, session migration, horizontal scaling\n\n\nQ2 2026\nEnterprise Auth\nOAuth 2.1 + PKCE GA, SAML/OIDC IdP integration\n\n\nQ2-Q3 2026\nServer Discovery\nMCP Server Cards (.well-known URL), standard metadata format\n\n\nQ3 2026\nAgent Communication\nAgent-to-agent coordination primitives (A2A integration)\n\n\nQ4 2026\nMCP Registry\nVerified server directory, security audits, SLA commitments\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  Elicitation and Sampling — Bidirectional Agent Interaction\n\n\nOne of MCP's powerful features is that servers can send reverse requests to clients (LLMs). Sampling lets MCP servers ask the LLM to generate text — used when an agent needs additional reasoning mid-task. Elicitation lets servers request direct input from users — used for human confirmation in long-running tasks or when additional information is needed.\n\nThese two features enable recursive agentic behavior. For example, a database migration server can detect schema changes, use Sampling to ask the LLM for impact analysis, and if deemed high-risk, use Elicitation to get final approval from the user — implementing a Human-in-the-Loop workflow. This is a key design pattern for balancing AI agent autonomy with human control.\n\n\n  \n  \n  Production MCP Adoption Checklist\n\n\nFor enterprise MCP adoption, consider this practical checklist organized around security, governance, and monitoring:\n\n\n\n\nPhase\nArea\nDetails\n\n\n\n\n1. Security\nAuth/AuthZ\nRemote servers: OAuth 2.1 + PKCE required. No hardcoded API keys — use Vault\n\n\n2. Security\nInput Validation\nPrompt injection defense, PII detection, minimum-privilege token scopes\n\n\n3. Governance\nServer Evaluation\nSource code audit, version pinning, supply chain security verification\n\n\n4. Governance\nRate Limiting\nPer-user/agent request limits, cost tracking (per-request token logging)\n\n\n5. Monitoring\nObservability\nOpenTelemetry tracing, request/response logging, error rate dashboards\n\n\n6. Monitoring\nHealth Checks\nMCP server availability monitoring, session state tracking, auto-recovery\n\n\n\n\n\n  \n  \n  Conclusion — MCP Defines the Future of AI Agent Integration\n\n\nMCP is the first successful open protocol to standardize how AI agents interact with the external world. Built on the proven JSON-RPC 2.0 foundation, it cleanly abstracts three primitives — Tools, Resources, and Prompts — and extends to enterprise environments with Streamable HTTP transport and OAuth 2.1 authentication.\n\nIn H2 2026, stateless server operation, automatic discovery through MCP Server Cards, and agent-to-agent coordination with A2A will mature, evolving MCP from single tool connections into the foundational infrastructure for multi-agent orchestration. Organizations adopting AI agents should establish MCP as their default integration layer and design security and governance from day one — the most practical strategy for 2026.\n\n\n\n\nAI Disclosure: This article was planned by the ManoIT technical research team, drafted by an AI assistant (Claude), and technically reviewed and edited by professional engineers.\n\nSources: Model Context Protocol Official Specification, MCP 2026 Roadmap Blog, FastMCP GitHub, Auth0 MCP OAuth Guide, WorkOS MCP Enterprise Roadmap, The New Stack MCP Production Guide\n\nLast Updated: 2026-04-06 | Author: ManoIT Tech Blog\n\n\n\n\nOriginally published at ManoIT Tech Blog.\n\n\n            ","url":"https://dev.to/x4nent/complete-guide-to-mcp-model-context-protocol-in-2026-architecture-implementation-and-4a11"}
{"text":"  Why MCP Servers Eat Your Context Window Alive\nIf you’ve built AI agents that call MCP (Model Context Protocol) servers, you’ve probably noticed your token counts climbing fast. A single tool call that returns a JSON blob with 50 fields — when you only needed 3 — can consume thousands of tokens in one shot. Multiply that across a multi-step workflow and you’re burning context window before the agent has done anything useful.\nToken usage in AI agents isn’t just a cost problem. It’s a performance problem. Bloated context slows inference, increases error rates, and hits limits on models with smaller windows. For MCP optimization specifically, the problem is structural: most MCP servers are built to return complete, accurate data — not minimal data. That’s fine for humans reading output, but it’s wasteful when an LLM only needs a fraction of what comes back.\nThese 10 techniques address that gap directly. Some are architectural choices. Some are encoding tricks. A few, like TOON notation, can cut token usage by up to 98% on certain payloads. All of them are practical and applicable whether you’re building from scratch or tuning an existing multi-agent workflow.\n\nUnderstanding the Token Waste Problem in MCP Workflows\nBefore the fixes, it helps to know exactly where the waste happens.\n\nIn a typical MCP interaction, the agent sends a tool call, the MCP server fetches or processes data, and the result lands back in the context window. That result gets read by the model and informs the next step. The problem is that “the result” is often far larger than necessary.\nCommon sources of token bloat in MCP workflows:\n\nVerbose JSON responses — APIs return full objects with dozens of keys when you needed one value\nOver-specified tool schemas — Tool descriptions and parameter definitions add hundreds of tokens before any data arrives\nRedundant context passing — Prior tool results stay in context even when they’re no longer relevant\nPaginated data loaded all at once — Full datasets fetched when a subset would do\nUncompressed structured data — Standard JSON is readable but not token-efficient\n\nThe fix isn’t to use fewer tools. It’s to use them more precisely.\n\n\n1. Filter Tool Outputs at the Server Level\nThe single highest-leverage change you can make: return only the fields the agent actually needs.\nMost MCP servers pass through the full API response. A GitHub MCP call that returns a pull request might include author info, timestamps, branch names, reviewer lists, CI statuses, labels, and more — when the agent only needed the PR title and diff.\nThe solution is field filtering at the MCP server layer. Before the response hits the context window, strip it down to the fields your agent workflow requires. This can be as simple as a projection function that whitelists specific keys.\nFor a response that typically returns 50 fields, filtering to 3–5 relevant ones can reduce payload tokens by 80–90%.\n2. Run Computation Server-Side Instead of in Context\nThis is one of the most underused MCP optimization techniques: move computation to the server, not the model.\nA common anti-pattern looks like this: the MCP server returns a large dataset, the agent processes it in context (sorting, filtering, aggregating), and produces a result. You’ve paid for every token of that dataset.\nThe better pattern: expose aggregation tools in your MCP server that do the processing before anything enters the context window. Instead of get_all_sales_records, expose get_sales_summary_by_region. Instead of list_all_issues, expose count_open_issues_by_priority.\nThis is the principle behind many high-efficiency MCP designs. Code runs in milliseconds server-side and costs nothing in tokens. The same operation in context costs hundreds or thousands of tokens.\n3. Compress Tool Descriptions Without Losing Function\nEvery tool you expose to an agent carries a description and a parameter schema. On a complex MCP server with 20 tools, the combined schema can add 2,000–4,000 tokens to every request — even for tools the agent never calls.\nA few approaches to reduce this overhead:\n\nTrim verbose descriptions — Tool descriptions should be precise, not exhaustive. “Returns user profile” beats a four-sentence explanation.\nUse shorter parameter names — q instead of search_query_string saves tokens at scale\nRemove optional parameters from default schemas — Only expose the full parameter set when the tool is actually being invoked\nUse tool selection layers — For large tool catalogs, use a router that presents only the relevant subset of tools based on the current task\n\nTool schema compression alone can reduce per-request overhead by 30–60% in agents with large MCP tool catalogs.\n\n    \n      \n        ✗\n          VIBE-CODED APP\n        \n        Tangled. Half-built. Brittle.\n      \n      \n        ✓\n          AN APP, MANAGED BY REMY\n        \n            UIReact + Tailwind✓\n            APIValidated routes✓\n            DBPostgres + auth✓\n            DEPLOYProduction-ready✓\n          \n        Architected. End to end.\n      \n    \n    \n      Built like a system. Not vibe-coded.\n      Remy manages the project — every layer architected, not stitched together at the last second.\n    \n  \n\nMany MCP tools default to returning all available data. That’s a reasonable default for a generic API, but it’s a token drain for agents.\nThe fix is to build pagination awareness into your MCP layer and into your agent prompting:\n\nDefault to small page sizes (10–20 items) rather than returning everything\nExpose has_more flags so the agent can decide whether to fetch the next page\nLet the agent request additional data only when it determines it needs it — not preemptively\n\nAn agent looking for a specific customer record in a CRM doesn’t need all 5,000 records dumped into context. It needs the ability to search, get a small result set, and stop when it finds a match.\nThis requires the agent’s reasoning to include awareness of when “enough data” has arrived — but that’s a solvable prompt engineering problem, and the token savings are significant.\n5. Cache Tool Results Within a Session\nRedundant tool calls are a silent token drain. An agent that queries the same Notion database three times in one workflow pays full token cost each time — both for the call overhead and the repeated response content.\nSession-level caching at the MCP layer solves this. When a tool result hasn’t changed and the same query is made within a session, return the cached result without re-fetching and, crucially, without reinserting the full response into context.\nMore advanced implementations use a context cache reference — instead of re-inserting the full tool response, inject a compact reference like [Cached: customer_record_id_4821] and have the agent resolve it only if needed.\nSome model providers (like Anthropic’s prompt caching feature) offer native support for caching repeated context segments, which pairs well with MCP result caching.\n6. Use TOON Encoding for Structured Data\nTOON (Token-Optimized Object Notation) is a compact alternative to standard JSON for passing structured data through agent contexts. It’s not a universal standard — different implementations vary — but the core idea is consistent: represent structured data in formats that use fewer tokens without losing semantic content.\nStandard JSON is human-readable but token-heavy. Consider this comparison:\nStandard JSON:\n{\n  \"customer_id\": \"cust_4821\",\n  \"first_name\": \"Sarah\",\n  \"last_name\": \"Chen\",\n  \"account_status\": \"active\",\n  \"plan\": \"enterprise\"\n}\nTOON-style compact encoding:\ncust_4821|Sarah|Chen|active|enterprise\nWith a schema definition the agent already has in context, the compact version conveys identical information at a fraction of the token cost. For large datasets — think 100 customer records or 500 log entries — the savings compound dramatically. Some benchmarks show 90–98% token reduction on highly repetitive structured data when moving from verbose JSON to compact schema-aligned encodings.\nThe tradeoff is that the agent needs the schema to interpret the compact format. This works best for predictable, repeated data shapes — not one-off responses.\n7. Summarize Large Responses Before Injection\nWhen an MCP tool needs to return genuinely large content — a long document, a lengthy email thread, a full code file — don’t inject the raw content. Inject a summary instead.\nThis can work in two ways:\n\nPre-summarization at the MCP layer: The MCP server runs a lightweight summarization step before returning the result. For document retrieval tools, this means the agent gets a 200-token summary instead of a 4,000-token document.\nOn-demand detail retrieval: The agent receives a summary plus a reference ID. If it determines it needs the full content, it calls a separate get_full_content(id) tool. Most of the time, the summary is enough — and you’ve avoided the token cost on every call.\nThis pattern works especially well for AI agents that process documents or research content, where the agent often needs to triage many sources before deciding which ones merit full attention.\n8. Batch Tool Calls Where Possible\nEach MCP tool invocation carries overhead: the tool call itself, its parameters, and the response — all in context. For agents that make many sequential calls, this overhead accumulates quickly.\nBatching reduces this by combining multiple operations into one:\n\nInstead of get_user(id_1), get_user(id_2), get_user(id_3) — expose get_users([id_1, id_2, id_3])\nInstead of separate write calls for multiple records — expose a bulk write operation\n\nThis reduces not just the per-call overhead but also the number of reasoning steps the agent needs to take, which itself reduces context length.\nWhen designing MCP servers for multi-agent workflows, building batch variants of common tools from the start is worth the extra implementation time.\n9. Use Selective Context Injection by Agent Step\nIn multi-step agents, the entire conversation history — including all prior tool results — sits in context for every subsequent step. Early steps may have fetched data that’s irrelevant to later steps, but it still costs tokens.\nSelective context injection addresses this by pruning stale tool results from the active context as the agent progresses:\n\nTag tool results with a relevance scope (e.g., “needed for steps 2–3 only”)\nAt each step, strip results that fall outside the current scope\nMaintain a summary of what was done in prior steps rather than the full detail\n\nThis is more complex to implement but has a large payoff in long-running agents. A 10-step workflow that accumulates context without pruning can end up with 15,000+ tokens in context before the final step. With selective injection, you might hold that to 3,000–5,000 tokens throughout.\n10. Deduplicate and Normalize Tool Call Patterns\nThis one is about workflow design rather than server architecture.\nPoorly designed agent prompts often lead to redundant or overlapping tool calls. The agent fetches the same data multiple times, asks similar questions in different ways, or retrieves large contexts when targeted queries would work.\nDeduplication strategies:\n\nTrack what’s been fetched — Maintain a simple log in the agent’s working memory of what data has already been retrieved\nNormalize query patterns — If the agent tends to ask “what is the user’s plan?” and “is this user on enterprise?” separately, consolidate these into one structured lookup\nUse structured output for tool selection — Rather than letting the agent reason freely about which tool to call, provide a structured decision step that selects the minimum necessary tools for the current task\n\n\nThese prompt-level and workflow-level changes don’t require touching the MCP server at all, but they can meaningfully reduce redundant calls — and with them, redundant token costs.\n\nHow MindStudio Helps You Implement These Techniques\nBuilding token-efficient MCP workflows from scratch requires managing a lot of moving parts: tool schemas, response filtering, caching logic, context pruning. That’s substantial engineering overhead before you’ve solved your actual business problem.\nMindStudio’s no-code agent builder lets you configure multi-agent workflows visually, with built-in control over what data flows between steps. You can define exactly which fields pass from one step to the next — no code required — which directly applies techniques 1, 9, and 10 above.\nMindStudio also supports custom JavaScript and Python functions within workflows. That means you can implement server-side computation (technique 2), TOON-style compact encoding (technique 6), and response summarization (technique 7) as custom steps in your pipeline without standing up separate infrastructure.\nFor teams building agents that connect to external APIs and data sources — the exact scenario where MCP token bloat becomes a problem — MindStudio’s 1,000+ pre-built integrations handle the connection layer. You focus on optimizing what data flows through the agent, not on managing authentication and API plumbing.\nYou can also expose your MindStudio workflows as MCP servers, making them callable by other AI systems like Claude or custom agents built with LangChain or CrewAI — a useful option for teams building layered multi-agent architectures where token efficiency across the full stack matters.\nTry it free at mindstudio.ai.\n\nMeasuring the Impact: What to Track\nApplying these techniques is only useful if you’re measuring the right things. Token optimization without monitoring is guesswork.\nKey metrics to track for MCP token efficiency:\n\nTokens per tool call — Average input + output tokens for each MCP tool in your workflow\nContext window utilization — What percentage of the available context window is in use at each agent step\nTool call count per session — Are redundant calls happening?\nCost per workflow run — The bottom-line measure of whether optimization is working\n\nMost major model providers expose token counts in API responses. For production multi-agent systems, instrument your MCP layer to log token counts per tool call so you can identify which tools are the biggest contributors to context bloat.\nEven basic logging will usually reveal that 2–3 tools account for the majority of your token usage — and those are the ones worth attacking first.\n\nCommon Mistakes to Avoid\nEven with good intentions, a few implementation patterns tend to undermine MCP optimization efforts.\nOver-filtering responses — Stripping too many fields can leave the agent without information it needs, causing extra round-trips or reasoning errors. Always profile what the agent actually uses before deciding what to cut.\nCaching stale data — Session caches are only useful if the underlying data doesn’t change during the session. For real-time data sources, caching can introduce errors. Build in staleness checks or use caching only for reference data.\n\nCompressing once, forgetting forever — As your agent’s capabilities evolve, tool schemas and response structures change. Token optimization is maintenance work, not a one-time fix. Re-audit when you add tools or modify workflows.\nIgnoring prompt-side bloat — Most MCP optimization discussions focus on tool responses. But system prompts and few-shot examples can also consume thousands of tokens. Treat prompt compression as part of the same budget.\n\nFrequently Asked Questions\nWhat is MCP in the context of AI agents?\nMCP stands for Model Context Protocol, an open standard that defines how AI models interact with external tools and data sources. It provides a structured way for agents to call tools — like searching the web, querying a database, or reading a file — and receive results. MCP servers expose these tools; AI agents consume them.\nWhy do MCP tool calls use so many tokens?\nToken usage in MCP comes from multiple sources: the tool schema definitions sent with each request, the tool call parameters, and — most significantly — the response content returned by the tool. If a tool returns a full API response with dozens of fields when only a few are needed, all of those extra fields still consume context window space.\nDoes reducing token usage affect agent quality?\nDone correctly, no. The goal is to remove redundant or irrelevant tokens, not useful information. An agent that receives a clean, filtered 200-token response will generally reason more accurately than one that has to parse through 3,000 tokens of noise to find the 200 tokens of signal it actually needs. Focused context often improves performance.\nWhat is TOON encoding and is it a standard?\nTOON (Token-Optimized Object Notation) is a compact data encoding approach used in agent and MCP contexts to reduce token usage on structured data. It’s not a single universal standard — implementations vary — but the core idea is representing structured records in delimited or abbreviated formats rather than verbose JSON, when the agent has access to a schema that defines the structure. It can achieve very high compression ratios on repetitive structured data.\nHow do I know which MCP tools are consuming the most tokens?\nInstrument your MCP layer to log input and output token counts for each tool call. Most model provider APIs return token usage metadata in responses, which you can capture and aggregate. Look for tools with high average response sizes or high call frequency — those are your best optimization targets.\nCan these techniques be applied to non-MCP agent architectures?\nYes. Many of these techniques — selective context injection, response filtering, server-side computation, caching — apply to any tool-using agent architecture, whether it uses MCP, function calling, ReAct patterns, or custom tool implementations. The principles are general; MCP is just the delivery mechanism.\n\nKey Takeaways\n\nMCP token bloat is structural — MCP servers are built for completeness, not efficiency. Optimization requires deliberate design choices.\nFilter at the source — Returning fewer fields from tool responses is the single highest-leverage optimization.\nMove computation server-side — Code execution is free; tokens aren’t. Aggregate and process before data enters context.\nCompact encoding works for repetitive data — TOON-style representations can reduce token usage by 90%+ on structured datasets.\nMonitor and iterate — Token optimization is an ongoing process. Instrument your workflows, identify the biggest cost drivers, and target them specifically.\n\n\n    \n      \n        Remy doesn't build the plumbing.\n        It inherits it.\n      \n      Other agents wire up auth, databases, models, and integrations from scratch every time you ask them to build something.\n    \n    \n      WHAT REMY DOESN'T HAVE TO BUILD\n      \n        \n          200+\n          AI MODELS\n          GPT · Claude · Gemini · Llama\n          ✓\n        \n        \n          1,000+\n          INTEGRATIONS\n          Slack · Stripe · Notion · HubSpot\n          ✓\n        \n      \n      \n        \n          \n          MANAGED DB\n        \n        \n          \n          AUTH\n        \n        \n          \n          PAYMENTS\n        \n        \n          \n          CRONS\n        \n      \n    \n    Remy ships with all of it from MindStudio — so every cycle goes into the app you actually want.\n  \nImplementing even 3–4 of these techniques in a production multi-agent workflow typically cuts token usage by 50–70%. The full stack of techniques can push that much further — and the savings compound with every agent run. MindStudio gives you the tooling to apply many of these without building the infrastructure layer from scratch.
